---

2

---

Abstract  This thesis investigates the supply side of the housing market, focusing on how regulatory frameworks and market conditions influence housing supply responsiveness in metropolitan areas.   With high and rising house prices becoming a pressing issue, the role of supply constraints has drawn significant attention. Understanding the relationship between housing demand, supply responsiveness, and price dynamics is critical to addressing housing market challenges. The first article explores how housing supply elasticities vary under different regulations regimes and market conditions. I combine price data with local housing stock, local employ- ment and local building development for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway between 2003 and 2021 and estimate supply elasticities.   Results show an average supply elasticity of 1.9 percent, with Oslo having a much lower elasticity of 0.4 percent. Apartments display higher elasticity compared to detached- attached- and row- houses, aligning with urban den- sification strategies in many cities. The second article explores the ratio of house prices to replacement costs in six Norwegian cities. We also estimate land prices and profit margins to identify who earn on prices exceeding minimum profitable production costs.   The third article evaluates the impact of a 2008 policy change in Oslo that introduced size restrictions on apartments, examining its effects on prices in the different market segments.   Finally, the fourth article employs fundamental value models for 14 municipalities to investigate ge- ographical variations in housing market cycles.   We also use the gap between actual prices and fundamental values to predict the likelihood of market turning points. Together, these articles contribute to a better understanding of the drivers and constraints of housing supply, offering insights into the mechanisms that influence housing market sta- bility and affordability. 3

---

4

---

Norsk sammendrag  Artiklene i denne avhandlingen omhandler boligmarkedet, med fokus på hvordan regulering på boligutvikling og markedsforhold påvirker boligtilbudet i storbyområder.   Med høye og stigende boligpriser som en stadig mer presserende utfordring, har rollen til tilbudsbegren- sninger fått økende oppmerksomhet. Å forstå forholdet mellom etterspørsel, tilbudsrespons og prisdynamikk er avgjørende for å adressere utfordringene i boligmarkedet. Den første artikkelen utforsker hvordan tilbudselastisiteter varierer under ulike regu- leringsregimer og markedsforhold.   Jeg kombinerer prisdata med igangsatte og ferdigstilte boliger, lokal sysselsetting og lokal byggeutvikling for de 25 største kommunene i Norge mellom 2003 og 2021 og estimerer tilbudselastisiteter.   Resultatene viser en gjennomsnit- tlig tilbudselastisitet på 1,9 prosent, med Oslo som har en betydelig lavere elastisitet på 0,4 prosent.   Leiligheter viser høyere elastisitet sammenlignet med eneboliger, tomannsboliger og rekkehus.   Dette samsvarer med fortettingsstrategier i mange byer.   Den andre artikke- len undersøker forholdet mellom boligpriser og gjenanskaffelseskostnader i seks norske byer. Vi estimerer også tomtepriser og fortjenestemarginer for å identifisere hvem som tjener på priser som overstiger minimumskostnadene for lønnsom produksjon.   Den tredje artikkelen studerer virkningen av Leilighetsnormen som ble introdusert i 2008 i Oslo.   Normen satte størrelsesbegrensninger på nybygde leiligheter, og vi estimerer effektene på priser i ulike stør- relsessegmenter. Den siste artikkelen i avhandlingen beregner fundamentale boligprismodeller for 14 kommuner for å undersøke geografiske variasjoner i boligmarkedssykluser. Vi bruker også gapet mellom faktiske priser og fundamentale priser for å forutsi sannsynligheten for vendepunkter i boligprisutviklingen. Samlet bidrar artiklene til en bedre forståelse av drivere og begrensninger i boligtilbudet, og gir verdifull innsikt i mekanismene som påvirker boligmarkedets tilbud og utvikling. 5

---

6

---

1   Introduction to the thesis  1.1   Introduction  High and increasing house prices in metropolitan areas, along with the role of restrictions on housing supply, have been extensively debated in recent years. The responsiveness of housing supply is essential in determining whether increasing demand results in higher prices or more housing units. When prices exceed construction costs and rise due to increased demand, de- velopers in a competitive market will be motivated to build more homes. Thus, unresponsive supply stems from regulations on housing development or geographical constraints, or both. The focus of the thesis is the supply side of the housing market, and the first three articles address this topic. The fourth article calculates fundamental house prices and studies whether the gap between actual and fundamental prices can predict turning points. Table 1 provides an overview of the four articles in the thesis. The first article,   Is housing supply meeting demand:   the role of regulations , explores how housing supply elasticities vary under different regulatory regimes and market condi- tions.   I combine price data with local housing stock, local employment and local building development for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway between 2003 and 2021.   Using a shift-share instrument of local employment, the article measures supply elasticities for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway, focusing on two supply indicators: building work started and completed.   The findings reveal an average supply elasticity of 1.9 percent, with Oslo showing a significantly lower elasticity of 0.4 percent.   Additionally, the results reveal that apartments exhibit higher supply elasticity compared to row houses, detached houses, and attached houses.   This pattern aligns with urban densification strategies adopted in many cities. The second article in the thesis,   Linking housing Tobin’s Q to zoning, regulation, and land prices , investigates whether the Norwegian housing market produces single-family homes at prices aligned with replacement costs. Inspired by E. Glaeser and Gyourko (2018), we con- struct a housing Tobin’s Q (HTQ) for the housing market, defined as the ratio of house prices to minimum profitable production costs ( mppc ) from 2010 to 2020. The article documents substantial variations in HTQ over time within individual markets and across cities at a given point in time. To explore whether land owners or developers benefit from the gap between house prices and   mppc , we estimate regional land prices using tear-down sales and developer markups using accounting data. Finally, we compare the estimated Tobin’s Q with a measure of Norwegian cities’ efforts to facilitate housing construction. 7

---

In the third article,   The price effect of restrictions on residential building , we investigate the price effect of enforcing regulation on the share of small and large apartments being built in the inner city of Oslo. An apartment size regulation was implemented in Oslo in 2008. The regulation set a minimum of 35 square meters per apartment in the inner city. The regulation was intended to ensure that the stock of new units would consist of both small and large apartments to secure a mixed demographic structure.   Newly built apartments below the minimum size plummeted after the regulation was implemented in both the regulated inner city area and the rest of Oslo.   We use transaction data for apartments to investigate the effect of the regulation on apartment prices within a difference-in-difference framework. We find that the price of apartments below the minimum size of 35 square meters increased by 3.3 percent compared to the control size segment of 40-50 square meters after the implementation of the apartment size regulation.   We also examine the price effect on large apartments. Despite an increase in the number of large newly built apartments in the regulated inner city, we find that the prices of large apartments increased compared to the districts in Oslo that was not regulated.   We suggest that this price increase can partially be explained by higher demand for large apartments from families with children, in line with the intention of the regulation. The final article in the thesis,   From boom to bust:   Probabilities of turning points in house prices , empirically tests whether deviations from fundamental prices— defined as the price that can be explained by disposable income, the housing stock, and the user cost of housing— affect the probability of turning points in house price cycles on a regional level. We apply Bry and Boschan (1971)’s algorithm to identify booms and busts in house prices for 14 municipalities in 4 different regions in Norway, using quarterly data from 2003 to 2021.   A cointegrated vector autoregressive model is used to identify long-run drivers in local house prices.   We find that while house price cycles, in many cases, are similar for municipalities within the same region, there is considerable heterogeneity in the timing, duration, and amplitude of booms and busts in real house prices across the regions.   We also find substantial heterogeneity across regions in the response to long-run fundamentals, particularly in the response to a change in the user cost. The model is utilized to investigate if the real house price gap, the difference between actual and model-implied prices, affect the probability of turning points in house prices. We find that an increase in the price gap from 0 to 15 percent increases the probability of a peak (and an ensuing downturn) in house prices by 7 percentage points. 8

---

1.2   Background and related literature  1.2.1   The Norwegian housing market  In 2023, 76 percent of the households in Norway were home owners 1 . Housing is the largest part of households wealth and accordingly, housing market conditions will have a large impact on households’ wealth distribution. It has been an active policy goal that as many households as possible own their own housing. The recent years house prices have surged, especially in urban areas and particularly in Oslo.   In Oslo, the median price of a detached house in 2020 was nearly 18 times the median household income, compared to 8 times in Bergen and Trondheim and just 6 times in Kristiansand. 2   The high and increasing prices, especially in Oslo, has led to much debate about housing development and policies for helping lower- income households into the housing market. A considerable share of the policies debated are directed towards the demand side, such as mortgage regulations and alleviating requirements for obtaining loans.   Norway has a low proportion of publicly owned housing units, with the vast majority sold on the market through English auctions.   As a result, discussions have also included altering the market structure for a portion of newly developed units, such as increasing the share of publicly owned housing or developers entering rent-to-buy arrangements with buyers.   Both demand-side policies and changes in market structures can influence   who   can afford to purchase housing units in the city, and, if implemented extensively, may also impact demographic development. However, rising prices are a strong signal that demand is outpacing supply and that there is an insufficient number of housing units. Neither stricter mortgage regulations nor changes to market structure will resolve this underlying issue.   Addressing the problem requires targeted supply-side measures to boost housing development. The responsiveness of the supply side is critical in determining whether increased demand leads primarily to higher prices or to the construction of additional housing units. A well functioning supply side is essential for the housing market to function. In Norway, the federal government, municipalities, and private developers each have distinct responsibil- ities in housing development. The federal government sets national policies, frameworks, and technical regulations that guide the overall housing strategy. Municipalities are responsible for planning, zoning, and regulating building construction, ensuring alignment with local needs and guidelines within their jurisdiction.   Meanwhile, private companies finance and  1 Source: Statistics Norway, Table 11084.  2 Sources: Eiendom Norge (median house prices) and Statistics Norway (median household income at the county level, Table: 06946).  9

---

build new housing units.  1.2.2   Related literature  Increasing prices in metropolitan areas is not a Norwegian phenomenon, and there is a growing body of research on housing supply and the effect of housing supply constraints. Housing supply can be constrained both geographically - by steep terrain, water bodies and wetlands - and by regulatory framework as zoning laws, minimum lot size requirements and maximum building heights restrictions. One challenge in this research is to have comparable measures of housing supply restrictions over time and between different geographical areas. Saiz (2010) constructs a measure of unavailable land for all major metropolitan areas in the U.S. by exploiting data on terrain and water bodies.   Gyourko et al. (2008) and Gyourko et al. (2021) develop The Wharton Residential Land Use Index (WRI) that aims to capture the degree and variability of regulatory restrictiveness across U.S. metropolitan areas and the measures are comparable over time. Both the WRI and the elasticities estimated by Saiz (2010) are widely used in housing research. Measuring the direct effects of geographical and regulatory constraints on housing devel- opment and house prices is also challenging. One key difficulty is the potential endogeneity of regulatory frameworks, as argued by, among others, Davidoff (2015), Fischel (2002), and Saiz (2010).   Fischel (2002) introduces the "homevoter hypothesis", suggesting that local residents vote to hinder housing development to protect the property values, thereby shaping local policies and regulations. Saiz (2010) employs the WRI and finds that it is correlated with-demand side factors. Davidoff (2015) argues along the same lines. Additionally, as Mol- loy (2020) notes, regulatory environments vary significantly both across and within cities. This variability, combined with overlapping regulations, complicates efforts to isolate the impact of individual policies on housing outcomes. The relationship between strict regulations on housing development and rising house prices has recently received much attention (E. Glaeser & Gyourko, 2018; Gyourko & Krim- mel, 2021; Gyourko & Molloy, 2015; Jackson, 2018; Molloy, 2020; Quigley & Raphael, 2005; Zabel & Dalton, 2011). E. Glaeser and Gyourko (2018) construct a Tobin’s Q for the housing market, defined as the ratio of house prices to minimum profitable production costs ( mppc ).   The   mppc  encompasses construction costs, land costs and developer markups.   The land cost is set to 20 percent of total costs, as it is stipulated to be if there were abundance of land and lenient regulations.   A sustained Tobin’s Q significantly above unity suggests barriers to 10

---

investment, often due to regulatory constraints on land development.   They find a large variation in the Tobin’s Q for housing, and that the ratio is higher in areas that are subject to strict regulations.   Quigley and Raphael (2005) construct a regulatory index for cities within California based on a survey of city manager on land-use. Their finding reveal that stricter regulations are associated with higher prices, lower growth in the housing stock and lower price elasticity. Jackson (2018) also develops a regulatory index for cities in California. In line with previous literature he finds that stringent regulations are associated with high price levels.   However, he does not find that the strict regulations are drivers in booms and busts, while geographical constraints are.   He interprets this to be an indication that geographical constraints are better proxies for supply elasticities than regulations are. The difference between actual house prices and the counterfactual prices that would exist under more lenient regulations is often referred to as a regulatory- or zoning tax (E. Glaeser & Gyourko, 2003, 2018).   This zoning tax can be significant.   For instance, Gyourko and Krimmel (2021) estimate the zoning tax in San Francisco as the gap between land values at the extensive and intensive margins, amounting to approximately USD 400,000 for a quarter-acre lot. Zabel and Dalton (2011) measure the effect of minimum lot size restrictions in Massachusets and find an increase in house prices of up 20 percent on house prices. Cities with inelastic housing markets tend to experience sharper price escalations during housing bubbles, are more vulnerable to such bubbles, and endure longer periods of inflated prices (E. L. Glaeser et al., 2008). The supply elasticity measures vary both between and within metropolitan areas. Saiz (2010) estimates supply elasticities for all major metropolitan areas in the U.S., finding a range from 0.5 to 5.4. Quigley and Raphael (2005) focus on California and find that supply elasticities are lower in cities with stricter regulations. Baum-Snow and Han (2024) estimate within city variation in supply elasticities, concluding that this variation is greater than the differences observed between cities. Aastveit et al. (2023) find that supply elasticities have decreased on average in metropolitan areas in the U.S. over time, especially in areas where regulations have tightened over the period studied. The above mentioned authors use panel data for estimations. There is also a body of literature using time series to measure supply elasticities, see for instance Caldera and Johansson (2013), Green et al. (2005), and Malpezzi and Maclennan (2001). Caldera and Johansson (2013) documents larger supply elasticities in the Nordic countries and the U.S., while they find lower responsiveness in Continental Europe and the U.K. Inelastic housing supply has far-reaching implications. Strict regulation on housing de- velopment results in reduced housing development and accelerated house price growth (E. 11

---

Glaeser & Gyourko, 2018; Quigley & Raphael, 2005; Saks, 2008). The elevated prices impact wealth distribution across regions and generations, as shown by E. Glaeser and Gyourko (2018) and Eggum and Røed Larsen (2024). Furthermore, inelastic housing supply adversely affects employment growth, worker productivity, and national economic productivity. Saks (2008) make use of land use policies to construct an index of regulations, serving as a proxy for supply elasticities. Subsequently, the authors develop a model that illustrates how supply elasticity affects labor market outcomes. In line with the model, she finds that employment changes resulting from increased labor demand were 20 percent lower in areas identified as having strict regulations according to the index.   The reduced mobility of workers has a potentially large impact on economic growth.   Hsieh and Moretti (2019) finds that the in- creasing house prices in productive cities as San Fransisco, San Jose, and New York limits the number of workers who can live and work there, subsequently lowering overall growth in the US. D’Amico et al. (2024) argue that regulations keep construction projects small and thus inhibits growth in firm sizes in the construction sector.   As larger firms invest more in technology and have higher productivity, they claim that this reduces the productivity growth in the construction industry, an industry which has displayed a falling productivity development in recent years. Given these significant consequences and the ongoing trend of urbanization, understand- ing the elasticity of housing supply in various regional markets is vital for policymakers seeking to address housing market challenges effectively. A common approach to construct house price models and evaluating whether housing prices align with fundamental drivers is the inverse demand approach (Anundsen, 2019; Meen, 1990, 2001; Muellbauer & Murphy, 1997). In this framework, the households maximize utility with respect to housing services and other goods.   The inverted demand equation suggests that real house prices depend on real income, the housing stock and user cost, where the housing stock is treated as predetermined. Anundsen (2019) applies this method for Norway, Finland and the US. He finds that the coefficient for the user cost in Norway is higher than for the other countries and argue that this may be due to a high share of floating rates on mortgages in Norway. To assess turning points in the house price cycles, the algorithm developed by Bry and Boschan (1971) can be applied.   The algorithm contains censoring rules that define key parameters for detecting cycle dynamics:   ( i )   the window, which specifies the time span within which the algorithm searches for local minima and maxima,   ( ii )   the phase, which sets the minimum duration of growth or contraction to classify the period as a boom or bust, and 12

---

( iii )   the cycle length, which defines the minimum duration of a complete cycle, measured from peak-to-peak or trough-to-trough.   In the original formulation by Bry and Boschan (1971), the window and phase were both set to 6 months, while the cycle length was set to 15 months. Harding and Pagan (2002) later adapted the approach from monthly to quarterly data.  1.3   Supply of housing  Figure 1 illustrates supply and demand for housing, comparing scenarios with elastic and inelastic supply.   The x-axis represents the number of housing units, while the y-axis rep- resents house prices.   The demand curve slopes downward as higher prices make housing less affordable.   The supply curve is vertical up to the existing housing stock and remains so until prices reach the minimum profitable production cost,   mppc .   When prices exceed the minimum profitable production cost ( mppc ), developers have an incentive to construct additional housing units. In a market characterized by perfect competition, constant returns to scale, and the absence of regulation, the supply curve would theoretically be horizontal at   p   =   mppc . However, in practice, the supply curve is often assumed to be upward-sloping due to increasing marginal costs as land becomes scarcer. As emphasized in this thesis, strict regulations on housing development induces a steeper supply curve.   Zoning laws, height restrictions, density caps, and lengthy approval processes reduce the elasticity of the hous- ing supply.   These regulatory constraints not only limit the ability to build but also make the process of developing new housing units more expensive and time-consuming. Figure 1 depicts two distinct market scenarios. Panel (a) represents a market with inelastic supply, characterized by a steep supply curve.   In this scenario, housing supply shows minimal re- sponsiveness to price increases, leading to only modest changes in the quantity of housing units. In contrast, panel (b) illustrates a market with elastic supply, where the supply curve is flatter. Here, housing supply responds to rising demand, resulting in a greater increase in the number of new housing units. The first two articles in the thesis draw on stylized markets inspired by E. L. Glaeser et al. (2008). These markets represent distinct scenarios:   i )   a growing market with lenient regulations,   ii )   a growing market with strict regulations, and   iii )   a depressed market with falling demand. The markets can be illustrated by Figure 1. A growing market with lenient regulations is depicted in panel b) Elastic supply. Here, a shift in demand from   D I   to   D II   , leads to a substantial increase in the number of housing units, from   q I   to   q II   , and a relatively modest rise in price, from   p I   to   p II   .   Conversely, a growing market with strict regulations 13

---

Figure 1: Supply and demand of housing: stylized examples  is shown in panel a) Inelastic supply. A shift in demand from   D I   to   D II   results in a much smaller increase in housing units, from   q I   to   q II   , and a larger increase in price, from   p I   to  p II   . Booms and busts in house prices are often characterized by shifts in demand. Panel a) suggest that inelastic markets experience larger price fluctuations during boom and busts. A depressed market with falling demand can be illustrated with a demand shift to   D III   in panel b).   In this scenario, the decrease in demand drives prices below the   mppc .   No new units will be developed at this prices because it is not profitable. Consequently, the number of units remains fixed at the existing housing stock.  1.4   Data  Much of the same data is utilized across the four articles as can be seen in Table 1. Three of the articles rely on transactions data from Eiendomsverdi. The dataset covers transactions of housing units in the 20 largest municipalities in Norway between 2003 and 2021.   The data set includes details such as sale price, interior square meters, land area, building year, building type, and ownership type. This data is employed to estimate hedonic models in the first two articles and to control for composition effects in the third article. In the third article, the transaction data are linked to a dataset from the Norwegian tax registry, which includes annual observations on households’ balance sheets for the period 2004-2019. The data consist of balance sheet information, including income, debt, and wealth, as well as education, profession, age, gender, and household type. In particular, the dataset 14

---

shows the number of people, including the number of children, in the household. This allows us to analyze effects on different types of households buying apartments. In the second article, we utilize a dataset of transactions where the buyer is an organization or a firm.   This dataset serves to construct a measure of land prices and includes details such as the sale price, sale date, type of existing building on the plot (if any), postal code, purchasing organization, the number of new dwellings developed on the plot after the sale, and the number of existing buildings demolished. To estimate developers’ markups, we rely on data from the SAFE accounting database, provided by the consultancy Samfunnsøkonomisk Analyse (SØA). This dataset, derived from public records, contains annual accounts for all companies subject to accounting regulations from 2011 to 2020. Using this data, we calculate a markup measure for the 15 largest developers active in each city, defined as the ratio of total operating income to total operating costs across all companies. The rest of the data is at municipality or national level. At the municipality level we use price indexes obtained from Eiendom Norge, housing stock from Ambita, and employment, building work started and completed, and disposable income from Statistics Norway. At the national level we employ consumer price index, disposable income, construction cost index and employment by industry from Statistics Norway, construction costs from Norsk Prisbok, and tax deduction rates form The Norwegian Tax Administration.  1.5   Methodological approaches  Hedonic models  In article 1 and 2, we use transaction data as the source for house prices. Solely measuring average price or price per square meter based on transaction data can introduce measurement errors when trying to capture the underlying price developments within a city at any given time. These errors arise from composition effects, such as variations in the types of housing units traded during that period, whether due to differences in vintage or location. To address this issue, we employ hedonic pricing models to measure prices (Rosen, 1974). These models can account for a range of housing characteristics, with each coefficient reflecting the implicit price of a specific attribute. In the hedonic models used in these articles the characteristics that are included in the model are interior square meters, the square of interior square meters (to capture non-linear effects), zip code, building type, and the building’s age decile. 15

---

Instrumental variables  In article 1, a major challenge in estimating supply elasticities is that price are determined in at the intersection of supply and demand. Consequently, it is unclear whether price changes are driven by shifts in supply or demand, leading to potential bias in the estimator when using OLS regression. To solve this, I employ an instrumental variable (IV) approach using local employment to instrument for price in the supply equation. The instrument variable must be correlated with demand (relevance) and must not be correlated with the error term in the supply equation (exclusion restriction). To strengthen the exogeneity assumption, I take two key steps.   First, I use shift-share employment as the instrument (Bartik, 1991). This variable is constructed by combining the industry employment share in each municipality as of 2002 with national industry employ- ment growth rates from 2003 to 2019. Leveraging national variations in employment rather than municipality-specific employment fluctuations, which potentially are influenced by local housing supply shocks. Second, employment in the construction industry is excluded from the instrument.  Difference-in-difference analysis  In article three, we study the price changes in apartments affected by the size restrictions imposed in 2008.   As prices have increased much over the time period studied and both demand and supply of housing as been prone to various local and national shocks we cannot use a regular OLS specification with a dummy for the regulation as identification. We use the Difference-in-difference method for identification (Roth et al., 2023). In this article we want to study the price effect on two segments of apartments; apartments below 35 square meters and apartments above 80 square meters.   Thus, we have two treated groups and two suitable control groups.   The key assumptions for identification is the parallel trends assumption and the stable unit treatment value assumption (SUTVA). The parallel trends assumptions implies that the treated group and the control group in each case had parallel trends prior to the time of treatment and are assumed to continue with parallel trend had none of the groups undergone treatment.   Parallel trends pre-treatment can be checked by visual inspection and further tested with a Wald test. While the SUTVA means that there are no spillovers between treatment of different groups. 16

---

Cointegrated vector autoregressive model  In article 4, we study regional house prices and whether deviations between actual prices and fundamental prices can influence the probability of a turning point in house prices. As different cities in Norway have followed distinct price trajectories, it is interesting to study whether these trajectories are influenced by fundamental economic drivers such as income, housing stock, and user cost dynamics. Both house prices, income, and housing stock exhibit non-stationary behavior, meaning they follow a trend over time. The trends in these variables can vary across municipalities, making it challenging to visually determine whether house prices in different regions are driven by economic fundamentals. Despite the non-stationary nature of individual series, they may cointegrate, meaning that they exhibit a stable long-term relationship.   To test whether real house prices cointegrate with user cost and real disposable income relative to housing stock we use the test developed by Johansen (1988). Once we have established that one or more cointegrating relationship exists, we use a cointegrated vector autoregressive model (CVAR) to identify the long-run relationship between local real house prices and user cost and real disposable income relative to housing stock.   Based on the results from the CVAR model we calculate the implied fundamental house prices for each municipality.  1.6   Overview and synthesis of articles  1.6.1   Overview  The thesis consists of four articles, three focus on the supply side of the housing market, while the fourth examines whether deviations from fundamental prices affect the probability of turning points in house prices.  Is housing supply meeting demand: the role of regulations  The first article examines how housing supply elasticities differ under various regulatory regimes and market conditions. I combine price data with local housing stock, local employ- ment and local building development for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway between 2003 and 2021.   Using shift-share employment as instrument, the article measures supply elasticities for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway, focusing on two supply measures: building work started and completed. I incorporate three measures of regulation or facilitation of housing development:   i )  the Housing Construction Facilitation Ranking developed by Samfunnsøkonomisk Analyse 17

---

(SØA),   ii )   the area regulated for housing development per capita, and   iii )   I estimate the supply elasticity for Kristiansand, Oslo, and Stavanger. These cities correspond to the three stylized markets outlined in Section 1.3: a growing market with lenient regulations, a growing market with strict regulations, and a declining market.   Consistent with the model, the results indicate that Oslo has a supply elasticity well below the national average, reflecting its constrained housing supply. Kristiansand, by contrast, exhibits a higher elasticity, suggesting a more responsive housing supply. In Stavanger, the estimated supply elasticity is also below average; this appears to reflect a decline in demand and, consequently, cost-adjusted prices, reaching levels where new development is no longer profitable. I also measure supply elasticities per building type to check if there are differences across building types. I find that apartments display higher elasticity compared to row, detached, and attached houses, aligning with urban densification strategies in many cities.   Lastly, I conduct a back-of-the-envelope calculation to estimate how many additional housing units Oslo would have obtained if the city had exhibited the same supply elasticity as the average of the other municipalities in the sample.  Linking housing Tobin’s Q to zoning, regulation, and land prices.  Coauthored with Andreas Benedictow and Erling Røed Larsen.  In the second article we construct a Housing Tobin’s Q (HTQ) for the Norwegian housing market, focusing specifically on detached homes in six major cities.   We start out with replicating E. Glaeser and Gyourko (2018) using observed house prices as the numerator and   mppc   as the denominator. Furthermore, we estimate house prices as if they were of a newer vintage using a hedonic model. Additionally, we incorporate data from tear-down sales and accounting data for development firms to measure land prices and profit margins in the selected cities.   This approach allows us to investigate who benefits from the gap between new house prices and   mppc , shedding light on the distribution of economic gains within the housing market. We observe substantial regional differences in HTQ across Norway and find that high HTQ appears to be associated with supply-side obstacles. Our findings suggest that policy plays a significant role, as restrictive building or zoning regulations are linked to house prices well above the minimum profitable construction cost.   Additionally, we find evidence indicating that landowners in highly regulated areas profit from the gap between house prices and replacement costs.   Oslo stands out with high and increasing HTQ throughout the study 18

---

period. Moreover, we observe notable variation in land costs across municipalities, with Oslo exhibiting the highest land prices, while Kristiansand and Drammen have the lowest.  The price effect of size restrictions on residential building  Coauthored with Jeanette Fjære-Lindkjenn  The third article investigates the impact of the “Apartment Size Norm” introduced in the inner city of Oslo in 2008 on the price per square meter of affected apartment sizes. A minimum size of 40 square meters per apartment was set in 2008, later reduced to 35 square meters in 2013. There were also minimum and maximum thresholds for the share of other size segments, including a requirement that at least 50 percent of apartments be larger than 80 square meters, which was lowered to 40 percent in 2013. The regulation aimed to enhance housing quality and promote the construction of a more diverse range of apartments to meet the needs of various demographic and social groups.   In particular, it sought to encourage the development of larger apartments suitable for families with children. Using transaction data on residential apartments and a difference-in-difference framework, we analyze the policy’s impact on apartment prices, focusing on both smaller units (below the size threshold) and larger units (above 80 square meters).   Additionally, by merging transaction data with tax records, we explore whether the policy influenced the demographic composition of buyers in Oslo’s inner city, particularly families with children. Construction of new apartments smaller than 35 square meters plummeted after the im- plementation of the apartment size regulation, both within the regulated inner-city area and throughout the rest of Oslo. The results reveal that the regulation led to a 3.3 percent increase in square meter prices for apartments below 35 square meters, with apartments between 40 and 50 square meters serving as the control group.   For larger apartments, the regulation successfully increased supply within the regulated area. Contrary to initial expectations, the prices of the largest apartments rose compared to the control group — apartments of the same size in Oslo’s unregulated areas. Aggregate data on household types by district show an increase in the number of families living in the inner city.  From boom to bust: Predicting turning points in house prices based on deviations from equilibrium.  Coauthored with Jeanette Fjære-Lindkjenn  In the final article in the thesis, we use municipality-level data from Norway to analyze the drivers of local housing cycles through a cointegrated vector autoregressive (CVAR) model 19

---

(Johansen, 1988).   We examine how the gap between fundamental and actual house prices influences the likelihood of peaks and troughs in housing markets. We begin by identifying booms and busts in house prices for Norway as a whole and for 14 of the largest municipalities between 2003 and 2021. To do this, we apply the algorithm developed by Bry and Boschan (1971) and Harding and Pagan (2002).   Next, we employ a CVAR model to estimate the relationship between real house prices and key fundamentals in each municipality.   These fundamentals include the real user cost, real disposable income, and housing stock.   Using this framework, we derive fundamental house prices based on the evolution of these underlying factors and the long-run coefficients estimated by the model.   Finally, we conduct a panel regression to evaluate how deviations between actual and fundamental house prices (the real house price gap) affect the probability of turning points in housing cycles. Our analysis reveals significant variation in housing cycles across municipalities and re- gions in Norway over the study period. The impact of fundamentals on long-run house prices varies across regions, particularly in response to changes in the real user cost, defined as the tax-adjusted real mortgage rate. For Norway as a whole, a one-percentage-point increase in the real user cost is associated with a 9.9 percent decline in real house prices in the long run. In Oslo and its surrounding municipalities, the sensitivity to user cost changes is higher than the national average. In contrast, municipalities in the remaining eastern region display a lower response to user cost changes.   The elasticity of real house prices with respect to disposable income is more consistent across regions, with values ranging from 1 to 2. This implies that a 1 percent increase in disposable income per housing unit is associated with a 1–2 percent increase in real house prices in the long run. Our panel regression results indicate that the real house price gap significantly increases the likelihood of a housing market peak. Specifically, an increase in the price gap from 0 to 15 percent raises the probability of a peak by 7 to 9 percentage points.  1.6.2   Synthesis and contribution to literature  The articles are interconnected. First and foremost, they are all empirical articles using data on the Norwegian housing market between 2003 and 2021. The three first articles are focused on supply side factors and the findings are in line with each other. The first article contributes to the literature on supply elasticities (Baum-Snow & Han, 2024; Quigley & Raphael, 2005; Saiz, 2010), confirming heterogeneous elasticities across municipalities. The article further advances the literature by exploring the timing of prices within supply models. I apply quarterly data from 2003 to 2019 and two supply side measures: 20

---

---

Building work started and Buildling work completed. This approach enables a comparison of supply elasticities based on Building work started using contemporaneous prices and Building work completed using lagged prices. The results reveal consistent findings across these two supply measures. Moreover, the analysis demonstrates that in the specification using Building work completed and lagged prices, the exogeneity of the lagged price cannot be rejected. This allows for the estimation of price elasticity of Building work completed using ordinary least squares (OLS). The OLS approach offers greater flexibility compared to instrumental variable (IV) methods, facilitating richer interactions and more nuanced analyses. The first article also contributes to the literature by providing estimations of supply elasticities across different building types, adding to research on urban densification.   The findings reveal significant variation in supply elasticity among housing types, with apartments exhibiting higher elasticity compared to other housing categories. These results align with urban planning strategies in many municipalities, which prioritize increasing housing density as part of their development goals. The second aricle contributes to the literature by replicating E. Glaeser and Gyourko, 2018 using Norwegian data and expanding their analysis with regional land prices and building industry markups.   The focus is on new home prices rather than second-hand homes, and we examine the residual gap between these prices and the   mppc .   We also investigate who benefits from the gap — landowners or developers. We estimate markups in developing firms across cities using accounting data from 2013 to 2021. Additionally, we estimate land prices based on tear-down sales in six cities. These two first articles, present three examples of cities; growing market with lenient regulations, growing market with strict regulations and a falling market, inspired by E. Glaeser and Gyourko (2018).   Kristiansand, which serves as the Norwegian example of a growing market with lenient regulations, demonstrates higher supply elasticity compared to the national average. Additionally, Kristiansand has a Housing Tobin’s Q below 1, and land prices are the lowest in our sample.   Land cost as share of total costs was 15 percent on average throughout the period studied. In contrast, Oslo represents a growing market with strict regulations. When measuring supply elasticity, Oslo displays a lower supply elasticity than the national average, at 0.4 percent. Housing Tobin’s Q remains above 1 and continues to rise over the period studied. Meanwhile, there is no increasing trend in the number of units reported in building work started. Furthermore, Oslo has the highest land prices in our sample, with land cost shares varying between 30 and 43 percent of total costs, with an average of 36 percent, during the period. 22

---

Stavanger represents an example of a depressed market with falling demand, also displays a low supply elasticity.   Cost-adjusted housing prices fell by 25 percent between 2013 and 2019.   Building activity, as measured by building work started, plummeted in 2014 and showed no signs of recovery throughout the study period. Although local employment—and consequently housing demand—has increased since 2013, building activity has not followed suit. The total housing supply is inherently asymmetric; while it can increase, it is constrained downward by the existing housing stock. Following the oil price fall in 2014, housing demand may have dropped below the level of available housing stock.   Although demand has since begun to recover, it may take time to catch up to the existing stock. Land prices in Stavanger were high — nearly on par with those in Oslo — before 2014. While land prices have declined somewhat since, they remain high compared to cities like Kristiansand. From a developer’s perspective, the total development costs, including land prices, may still not make it profitable to develop new housing in a large scale. The third article complements the two above, by delving into the impact of one specific regulation in Oslo —the apartment size restriction. The apartment size restriction imposed a minimum size requirement of 35 square meters for apartments. The article focuses on how this regulation affected house prices in this price segment, with results aligning with expectations based on prior literature. This article also contributes to the literature of documenting costs of regulations on housing development.   We employ a difference-in-difference framework, where apartments below 35 square meters are treated and using other apartments within the same city as the control group.   Our study advances this literature by identifying a causal relationship between the construction halt of apartments smaller than 35 square meters and their relative price increase. The fourth article contributes to the research examining the long-run drivers of house prices and house price cycles. We construct fundamental prices for municipalities in Norway, based on Anundsen (2019), and connect fundamental prices to the turning points in actual house prices.   The article contributes to research on regional house price models, and we document variations in long-run relationships between real house prices and fundamentals at the municipality level, especially across geographically distant regions. We also find that the user cost coefficient is higher in many of the areas with strict regulations on housing development.   This resonates with the discussion in Section 1.3 on larger price effects in regulated areas from demand shifts.   Finally, this article contributes to the literature on predicting house price cycles. We find that an increase in the price gap between actual prices and model-implied fundamental prices from 0 percent to 15 percent raises the probability of a peak in house prices (and a subsequent downturn) by 7 percentage points. 23

2   Is housing supply meeting demand: the role of regula- tions  28

---

Is housing supply meeting demand: the role of regulations ∗  Nini Barth   †  December 2024  Abstract  This paper explores how housing supply elasticities vary under different regulations regimes, and market conditions. I combine price data with local housing stock, local employment, and local building development for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway between 2003 and 2021.   Using Bartik type employment as instrument, the paper measures supply elasticities for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway, focusing on two supply indicators: building work started and completed. These results reveal an average supply elasticity of 1.9 percent, with Oslo having a much lower elasticity of 0.4 percent. Apartments display higher elasticity compared to detached, attached, and row houses, thereby aligning with urban densification strategies in many cities.  Keywords:   Housing supply, Government policy  JEL codes:   R31; R38  ∗ I express my gratitude to Clo´ e Garnache, Dag Einar Sommervoll and Andreas Benedictow for usefull comments. An earlier version of the paper was presented at The Urban Research Conference 2023 at OsloMet.  † School of Economics and Business, Norwegian University of Life Sciences and Housing Lab, Oslo Metropolitan University. Email: ninibart@oslomet.no  1

---

1   Introduction  The responsiveness of supply is essential in determining whether increasing demand results in higher prices or a larger number of housing units. When prices exceed construction costs and rise due to increased demand, developers in a competitive market will be motivated to build more homes.   Thus, unresponsive supply stems from regulations on housing development or geographical obstacles, or both (Saiz, 2010).   Unresponsive, or inelastic, supply has a wide range of consequences.   Cities with inelastic supply tend to experience steeper price increases during housing bubbles, are more prone to such bubbles, and the bubbles tend to persist for longer periods (E. L. Glaeser et al., 2008).   Inelastic housing supply leads to less development of new housing and higher house price growth (Saks, 2008).   These elevated prices and growth rates affect wealth distribution, both across regions and between generations (Eggum & Røed Larsen, 2024; E. Glaeser & Gyourko, 2018).   Additionally, inelastic supply significantly impacts employment growth, worker productivity, and national productivity (Accetturo et al., 2021; Ciccone, 2002; Hsieh & Moretti, 2019; Saks, 2008). In light of this context and the trend of increasing urbanization, it is key for policymakers to understand the elasticity of housing supply in different regional markets. In this paper, I present a simple framework of housing supply and demand to illustrate supply dynamics under different regulation regimes and market conditions. I introduce three stylized examples: a growing market with lenient regulations, a growing market with strict regulations and a falling market. Subsequently, I use quarterly data on local prices, employ- ment, building work started and building work completed for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway to measure supply elasticities.   I start out with measuring the average supply elasticity for these municipalities using a Bartik instrument for employment to instrument for prices in the supply equation (Bartik, 1991). I apply two measures of housing supply; building work started and building work com- 2

---

pleted, and measure supply elasticities using both measures. As building processes can take several years, the relevant prices to be used in the estimations will differ depending on which measure of supply is used. The number of units in building work completed at time   t   natu- rally depends on the number of units for which building work has been started in previous periods. Thus, the number of units for which building work has been completed depends on house prices in previous periods, and I test for different lags in price. Based on statistical significance and adjusted R-square, I use a five-period lag for price in the supply equation when measuring supply with building work completed.   Moreover, I assume that Building work started depends on contemporaneous prices. The estimated supply elasticities are not significantly different when measured using either building work started or building work completed, under both IV and OLS specifications for the latter. Additionally, I cannot re- ject that the lagged price is exogenous in the IV specification of building work completed. I proceed with measuring the supply elasticities of building work completed and lagged prices using OLS specification, as this offers greater flexibility to incorporate interaction terms. I expand the model with a measure of facilitation, using the Housing Facilitation Rank developed by Benedictow et al. (2022), and estimate the supply elasticity associated with municipalities displaying low, medium, and high degree of housing development facilitation. Additionally, I include square meters regulated for housing per capita as an indication of fa- cilitation of housing development. I also measure the supply elasticity for Oslo, Kristiansand, and Stavanger, which resemble the three stylized markets presented in the empirical frame- work. In line with the model, I find that the supply elasticity in Oslo is well below average and that Kristiansand posits a higher elasticity. The estimated supply elasticity in Stavanger is also lower than the average; it appears that demand and, thus, the cost-adjusted price has fallen to a point where development is not profitable. Lastly, I measure the supply elasticities by building type. The population in the 25 largest municipalities in Norway has grown by 27 percent on average in the time period studied. 3

---

The local authorities in many of the municipalities have upzoning as a goal, which implies that they facilitate more dense building and the share of built apartments is increasing. I find an average supply elasticity of 1.9 percent on average for all the municipalities in the sample and a supply elasticity of 0.4 percent for Oslo.   Additionally, when measuring supply elasticity by building type, I find that the supply of apartments has a higher supply elasticity compared to detached, attached, and row houses, which is in line with upzoning policies. This article contributes to the literature on measuring supply elasticities (Aastveit et al., 2023; Baum-Snow & Han, 2024; Quigley & Raphael, 2005; Saiz, 2010).   The findings of heterogeneous supply elasticities across municipalities are in line with those of Saiz (2010). Saiz (2010) measures supply elasticities ranging between between 0.5 and 5.4, while the range of supply elasticity I measure is between 0.4 and 0.7 in Oslo and 2.5 percent in Kris- tiansand. Aastveit et al. (2023) document a decline in supply elasticities between the two periods 1996–2006 and 2012–2019 in U.S. metropolitan areas. Their findings indicate that the largest reduction in supply elasticity occurred in metropolitan areas where regulations have been tightened between the two periods. These results align with the findings of this article, which show that municipalities with high facilitation of housing development exhibit significantly higher supply elasticities compared to those categorized as medium facilitation. This article is also closely related to Baum-Snow and Han (2024), who find differences in supply elasticities across neighborhoods within a municipality. The results on variations in supply elasticities between housing types support the notion of neighborhood-level differ- ences in supply elasticity. This is because neighborhoods are often characterized by different building type compositions and zoning regulations specific to each type. The article also contributes to the literature by examining the timing of prices within the supply models. While previous studies in many cases use a panel where they observe changes in housing stock and house prices over a time span of a few years, I have access to data on 4

---

a quarterly basis between 2003 and 2019. I compare the supply elasticities of building work started with respect to contemporaneous prices and of building work completed with respect to lagged prices and show that the results are very similar using the two supply measures. I also demonstrate that in the specification with building work completed and a lagged prices structure, I cannot reject that the lagged price is exogenous.   Thus, the price elasticity of building can be estimated with OLS, which is more flexible than the IV approach and allows for more interactions. The article contributes to the literature with estimations on supply elasticities for different building types. This is also related to the literature on urban densification (Ahlfeldt & Barr, 2022). The findings show variation in supply elasticity across different housing types, with the elasticity of supply for apartments being higher than that for other types of housing. This aligns with the urban planning strategies of many of the municipalities, which focus on increasing housing density. The paper is organized in the following mannerer: Section 2 presents the empirical frame- work applied, section 3 presents the institutional setting and data, section 4 presents the results. In Section 5, I do a back-of-the-envelope exercise and estimate the number of new housing units in Oslo if its supply elasticity matched the average of other large municipalities in Norway. Section 6 concludes.  2   Empirical framework  First, I present a simple framework of supply and demand for new housing 1 . I assume that landowners decide whether to develop their land based on the difference between house prices and construction costs. For simplicity, I assume a one-period setup, where landowners choose to develop their land as long as prices exceed construction costs,   p > c . The supply of new  1 See for instance Malpezzi and Maclennan (2001) for a similar setup.  5

---

housing, denoted as   h s , is represented by Equation 1:  h s   =   a s   +   β ( p   −   c )   (1) where   h s   is directly related to the price–cost gap, ( p   −   c ), which implies that new hous- ing is supplied only when prices exceed construction costs, making development profitable. Conversely, when prices fall below construction costs, no new construction takes place, so  h s   > = 0 . The parameter   β   captures the sensitivity of new housing supply to this price-cost gap. The demand for new housing,   h d , is expressed by Equation 2:  h d   =   a d   +   δp   +   d   (2) where   h d   also depends on price,   p , and a demand shifter,   d .   δ   has a negative sign, as a higher price reduces the demand for houses. The demand shifter,   d , can represent variables such as local amenities, the current housing stock, employment opportunities, or demographic trends. Price is set in the intersection between supply and demand. By combining equations 1 and 2, we derive the reduced-form price equation:  p   =  ( a d   −   a s ) +   d   +   βc β   −   δ   (3) The price,   p , depends on demand,   d , and construction costs,   c .   The variables are in log form; thus   β   can be interpreted as the supply elasticity and   δ   as the price elasticity of demand. Inserting for   p   in either Equation 1 or 2 yields the reduced-form of new houses,   h :  h   =   δ β   −   δ   ( βc   −   a s ) +   β β   −   δ   ( d   +   a d )   (4) 6

---

To demonstrate how regulations and demand play a part in the model, three examples are presented below: a growing market with lenient regulation, a growing market with strict regulation, and a depressed market with falling demand 2 . The first case involves a growing market, with lenient regulations on housing develop- ment.   Under this scenario, assuming constant returns to scale, the price elasticity,   β , will approach infinity and landowners will produce houses until prices equal construction costs. This simplifies the reduced-form price equation, Equation 3, to  p ′   =   c   (5) The reduced-form for new housing, Equation 4, reduces to  h ′   =   a d   +   δc   +   d   (6) In this market the developers will supply houses until the price equals the construction costs, and number of new housing units will be determined by demand at   p   =   c . In most cases there may be supply constraints. Additional costs due to geographical or regulatory constraints may lead to increasing marginal costs for landowners and developers. Saiz (2010) shows that the price sensitivity to demand shocks increase when developable land becomes more scarce. This means that we have an upward sloping supply curve with   β  greater than 0 but not approaching infinity. More stringent regulations imply a lower price elasticity,   β . Second, to illustrate a growing market with strict regulations and binding supply con- straints, we assume that housing development is primarily determined by regulations and permits rather than being driven by the relationship between house prices and construction  2 E. Glaeser and Gyourko (2018) illustrate how these three types of markets play out in a supply and demand figure, and present data on prices relative to building costs together with building work started for San Francisco, Atlanta, and Chicago as examples of the three markets.  7

---

costs. In this scenario, new housing development is determined by   h p , which represents the supply of housing determined by the number of permits issued or regulatory constraints. Consequently, the general supply equation, Equation 1, is updated in the following manner:  h ′′  s   =   h p   (7) Setting the new supply equation, Equation 7, equal to the demand equation, Equation 2, and solving for the price yields:  p ′′   =   − a d   −   d   +   h p  δ   (8) Provided that   p ′′  ≥   c . Inserting for   p ′′   in the demand equation for new housing, yields the reduced-form for new housing units:  h ′′   =   h p   (9) In this fully regulated market, developers do not respond to price differences between house prices and construction costs and the supply elasticity is zero,   β   = 0. Instead, housing development is driven by the availability of permits.   Consequently, the reduced-form for new housing units, Equation 9, remains identical to the supply equation, Equation 7.   As Equation 8 shows, the prices in this market are not dependent on construction costs. In the third case, a depressed market with a decreasing demand for housing, the demand for housing falls below the existing housing stock, with prices dropping below total costs  p < c .   Consequently, no new housing is developed.   In this scenario, the supply equation from the general model, Equation 1, equals zero and housing supply is equal to the existing housing stock: 8

---

h ′′′  s   = 0   (10) Setting demand, Equation 2, and supply, Equation 10, equal to each other yields the following reduced-form price equation:  p ′′′   =   − a d   −   d δ   (11) In this market, prices are not dependent on construction costs either,   β   = 0 and   h s   = 0, and there is no new development.   The total housing supply equals the existing housing stock. Although the examples above are stylized, they offer some insight into how markets respond to shifts in the supply and demand functions.   None of the largest municipalities in Norway have experienced a decline in population. In fact, all the largest municipalities in Norway are witnessing significant population growth.   If we were to find an instance of example 3 — a depressed market — among the municipalities studied, it could be Stavanger. The regional crisis following the decline in oil prices in 2013–2014 may have temporarily driven housing demand down to the level of existing stock. Oslo — with its growing market, strict regulations, and house prices well above replacement costs — may resemble example 1, where construction depends on permits. This would suggest that rising construction costs would not affect house prices, but rather affect land prices or developer profits. Kristiansand is often cited as an example of a municipality that has consistently promoted and supported housing development over time, thereby making it the closest example we have to example 2.   The city has lenient regulations and a growing market.   In Benedictow et al. (2022), Kristiansand was ranked as number 1 in the total Housing Construction Facilitation Ranking of the 20 largest municipalities in Norway and Oslo at the bottom 20, which supports the choice of these two municipalities as examples. 9

---

Empirical implementation  In the empirical implementation, supply of new housing units is represented by the following equation:  h n m,t   =   α 1   +   βp c m,t − l   +   t q   +   M m   +   ϵ   (12) where   h n mt   is the log supply of new housing in municipality,   m , at time   t .   I presume  h n   ≥   0. Both housing supply and price measures are in log form: thus,   β   can be interpreted as the price elasticity of supply.   p c m,t − l   is the log construction cost adjusted price index at time   t   −   l , where   l   is a lag. I do not have access to construction costs at the regional level and, thus, all prices are deflated with the same construction cost index.   t h   represents period fixed effects (quarterly),   M m   municipality represents fixed effects, and   ϵ   is the error term. The main variables used to measure new housing supply,   h n m,t , is building work started and building work completed. I assume that building work   started   is influenced by the price developments in the current period.   To model the supply of new housing, where building work   completed   is the primary variable of interest, we need to account for the fact that the completion of housing units depends on when building work started. Therefore, the supply of new housing (building work completed) is modeled as a function of lagged house prices. I test for different lags in prices in Equation 12, both for building work started and building work completed. The results are presented in Table B.2. and Table B.3 in Appendix B. To test the hypothesis that regulation reduces supply elasticity, I extend the model by incorporating measures of how municipalities regulate or facilitate housing development in the model, represented by   X m   in Equation 13.   First, I include the Housing Construction Facilitation Ranking (HCFR) constructed by Samfunnsøkonomisk Analyse (SØA) for the Co-operative Housing Federation of Norway (NBBL). I divide the municipalities into three groups based on their rating on the HCFR and include an interaction with the lower and 10

---

medium facilitated group, thereby leaving the group classified as highly facilitated to be the base group. The HCFR is described in Section 3. Second, I include the area regulated for housing per capita as an indication of regulatory stringency, where a larger area regulated for housing per resident suggests more lenient regulations and a smaller area indicates stricter regulatory constraints. Third, I include an interaction with the three cities — Kristiansand, Oslo, and Stavanger — which are the closest to resembling the three stylized examples of a growing market with lenient regulations, growing market with strict regulations, and a declining market, respectively.  h n m,t   =   α 1   + ( β   +   b   ∗   X m ) p c m,t − l   +   ψt h   +   φM m   +   ϵ   (13) The regulation indicators and cities, denoted by   X m , are interacted with the price in turn. Accordingly, the supply elasticity is   β   for the base group and   β   +   b   ∗   X m   for the other groups.  2.1   Bartik-type instrument construction  As price and quantity are jointly determined by the interaction between supply and demand, simply running Equation 12 with an OLS regression is likely to produce biased price elasticity estimates,   β .   The key challenge to identify unbiased   β ’s is to find an exogenous factor that shifts local demand for housing, without affecting supply factors as productivity in construction, construction costs, or land availability for example. I apply the 2SLS method and use a Bartik-type instrument of employment to instrument for price changes in the supply equation, as done by Baum-Snow and Han (2024), and Quigley and Raphael (2005). The reduced-form for the cost-adjusted price is obtained by subtracting the log construc- tion cost from both sides of Equation 3:   p   −   c   =   p c   =   a d − a s  β − δ   +   δ β − δ   c   +   d β − δ   . Equation 14 is the first stage and the empirical equivalent: 11

---

p c mt   =   κ   +   υ 1 l t   +   υ 2 l t − 1   +   υ 2 l t − 2   + + υ 2 l t − 3   +   M m   +   y q   +   ζ   (14) Employment in the current and lagged periods represent the demand shifters and   d β − δ   is approximated by   υ 1 l t   + υ 2 l t − 1   + υ 2 l t − 2   + υ 2 l t − 3 . As I do not have access to construction costs at a regional level, the potential differences in construction costs,   c , between municipalities are captured by the municipality fixed effects,   M m , and cost development over time is captured by the time fixed effects,   y q . The first stage results are presented in B.4 and B.5 in Appendix B and reveal that both the level and growth of Bartik employment have an effect on the cost-adjusted prices. The identification strategy is a two-step strategy; Bartik employment is used instead of actual employment in Equation 14 to instrument for price in Equation 12.   The reason Bartik employment is used instead of employment is to ensure that supply shocks — such as changes in construction productivity, costs, or land size — do not affect the instrument. Bartik employment is independent of supply conditions, as it uses industry shares in the beginning of the period and growth rates for the national industry employment. Additionally, employment in the construction industry is excluded from the aggregate, which is also a measure to ensure that supply-side factors do not affect the instrument. To construct the Bartik instrument, I use employment shares at two-digit NACE in 2002 in each municipality as the base.   The industry employment levels per municipality are then developed through the use of national growth rates in employment at the correspond- ing industries from National Accounts. Lastly, these industry measures of employment are summed up to the aggregate Bartik-type employment per municipality. Bartik employment instrument per municipality is displayed with actual employment in Figure B.1.in Appendix B. To ensure that Bartik employment explains the observed employment, observed employ- 12

---

ment is regressed on Bartik employment, municipality fixed effects, and time fixed effects. The results are presented in Table A.1 in Appendix A. Goldsmith-Pinkham et al. (2020) rec- ommends several empirical tests to assess what drives the variation in the Bartik instrument; whether it is variation in the initial industry shares or variation in growth rates and what in- dustries (instruments) are dominant in the aggregate Bartik instrument. The empirical tests are presented in Appendix A. The tests show that it is the variation in the initial shares that drive the variation in the instrument, and that the industries Health and social work, Public administration, Education, Wholesale and retail trade, repair of motor vehicles, and Transport that are dominant in the instrument. I assess the first three industries — Health and social work, Public administration, and Education — to be unrelated to potential supply shocks in housing development. Parts of Wholesale and retail trade and repair of motored vehicles and Transport may be affected by supply shocks in the construction industry or housing development. However, my assumption is that employment in these industries are less likely to be affected by supply side shocks than their sales and turnover. Overall, I assess the Rotemberg weights to support the assumption of exogeneity and that Bartik employment is independent of   ϵ   in Equation 12.  3   Institutional settings and data  Institutional settings  In Norway, the municipalities hold responsibility for planning, zoning, and regulating build- ing construction. The time span from vacant land, or a site designated for redevelopment, to completed housing units may be a long one.   The first step is to regulate the land for housing development, which implies that the municipality must formally designate the land as suitable for residential use. Once the land is regulated for housing, detailed zoning plans for the land is presented and there is a consultation process before the zoning plans can be 13

---

approved.   Private interests may also propose changes in the zoning plans.   The next step in the building process is that the developer applies for general permission, which outlines the external parameters of the construction project. Typically, the developers have to sell approximately 50–60 percent of the housing units to be constructed to obtain a building loan. After receiving general permission and securing financing (such as building loans), the developer applies for project start-up permission. Once granted, the actual construction can begin.   The building process typically lasts between 12 and 24 months, depending on the type and size of the building. Once the housing unit is complete, the developer applies for provisional permission to use and then a certificate of completion. This process, while de- tailed, ensures that housing developments adhere to local regulations, community standards, and safety codes; however, it can also result in lengthy timeframes between project initiation and completion. The time municipalities spend on each step of the process is crucial for the ease and cost of housing development. The initial step—regulating land for housing—can be particularly time-consuming and may lead to significant capital costs for landowners. Samfunnsøkonomisk Analyse (SØA) has developed a Housing Construction Facilitation Rank (HCFR) for The Co-operative Housing Federation of Norway (NBBL) for all major municipalities in Norway.   The HCFR ranks the municipalities on how well they facilitate housing development (Benedictow et al., 2022), providing insight into which municipalities are better equipped to meet housing demand and stimulate construction. The ranking com- prises several indicators, which are grouped into two main subcategories:   facilitation and construction. The construction ranking includes both buildings completed and prices and, thus, is not suitable to include in the model. However, the facilitation ranking (FR) attempts to measure how effectively municipalities promote and support housing development. It fo- cuses on factors that influence how quickly and efficiently new housing can be developed, which directly affects how supply responds to changes in demand. The facilitation ranking is based on four indicators for time for processing building applications, three indicators 14

---

for costs associated with applying infrastructure investments, one indicator for digitalization if building applications, and three indicators of other types of building facilitation 3 .   The facilitation ranking for the 25 largest municipalities is presented in Table 1. By incorporating the FR into the model, it helps explain how well municipalities can respond to increased housing demand, with higher-ranked municipalities expected to exhibit a more elastic housing supply in response to rising prices.  Table 1   Housing Construction Facilitation Rank: Facilitation ranking (FR).  Municipality   Ranking 2020   Ranking 2020 High facilitation:   Bodø   1   Drammen   14 Sandnes   2   Oslo   15 Arendal   3   Moss   16 Lørenskog   4   Low facilitation:   Lillestrøm   17 Sandefjord   5   Trondheim   18 Bergen   6   Tromsø   19 Skien   7   ˚ Alesund   20 Kristiansand   8   Sarpsborg   21 Medium facilitation:   Stavanger   9   Larvik   22 Asker   10   Fredrikstad   23 Indre Østfold   11   Tønsberg   24 Bærum   12   Nordre Follo   25 Ullensaker   13  Source: The Co-operative Housing Federation of Norway, NBBL. Notes: The table reports the ranking of the the 25 largest municipalities in Norway on the Housing construction facilitation rank (HCFR) sub rank: Facilitation, obtained from NBBL. The decreasing score implies that the municipality assessed as the top facilitator of housing development is ranked as number 1. The high, medium, and low facilitation division is done by dividing the municipalities into three groups by their respective HCFR.  Data  The dataset consists of quarterly data from 2003 to 2019, encompassing the 25 largest mu- nicipalities in Norway. The data used is described in Table 2 and the municipalities included are presented in Table 1.  3 Share of approved applications, share of private planning proposals, and land reserves for housing development compared to historical population growth.  15

The data is for the municipality level and is collected for the 25 largest municipalities in Norway, except for the transaction data, which only is available for the 20 largest municipalities.  Statistics Norway provides data on new housing units under building work completed and building work started by building type and municipality on a quarterly basis. Building work started is tied to the issuance of a building permit, while building work completed is determined either by the granting of temporary permission to use or the formal certificate of building accomplishment. The time lag between building work started and building work completed will depend on the size of the project and the building type, and there are no available statistics on this time lag.   Large (and small) apartments projects will naturally take more time to build than row, detached, and attached houses. The Co-operative Housing Federation of Norway (NBBL) reports typical building durations of 12–18 months for row, detached, and attached houses and 18 months for an apartment project with approximately 50 units, 24 months for larger apartment projects, and 12–24 months for apartment projects in subsequent steps (where some ground work is already done). This corresponds with the lags used in the model in which building work completed is the supply measure. I rely on two sources for data on house prices. For the overall price development at the municipal level, I use price indexes from Eiendom Norge.   The accumulated change in the 16

---

construction cost-adjusted price index and the accumulated change in housing stock is shown in Figure 1. In most municipalities, real house prices have grown at a faster rate than the increase in housing stock.  Figure 1   Accumulated change in real house prices and housing stock. The dataset includes sell price, square meters, land area, and building type from 2003 to 2021 4 .
